# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

Perfect! I have successfully traced the trait obligation fulfillment chain in the Rust compiler. Here's a summary of my findings:

## Trait Obligation Fulfillment Chain

I've created `/workspace/answer.json` with comprehensive documentation of how Rust trait obligations are fulfilled:

### Key Components Found:

1. **ObligationForest** (`compiler/rustc_data_structures/src/obligation_forest/mod.rs`)
   - The core data structure that maintains a tree of outstanding trait obligations
   - Supports registration and processing of obligations
   - Implements two traits: `ForestObligation` (what can be stored) and `ObligationProcessor` (how to process them)

2. **FulfillmentContext** (`compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/fulfill.rs`)
   - Manages the fulfillment process using ObligationForest
   - Contains `FulfillProcessor` that implements `ObligationProcessor`
   - Tracks `PendingPredicateObligation` items and manages type inference interaction

3. **Trait Selection & Candidate Collection** (`compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/`)
   - **SelectionContext**: Core type resolution engine
   - **assemble_candidates()**: Collects possible implementations (trait aliases, impls, builtin traits, projections, etc.)
   - **assemble_candidates_from_impls()**: Searches the trait impl database with deep rejection filtering

4. **Impl Matching** (`compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/_match.rs`)
   - **MatchAgainstFreshVars**: Pattern matching using fresh type variables to check potential unification without affecting inference state

5. **Confirmation** (`compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/confirmation.rs`)
   - **confirm_candidate()**: Validates selected candidates through full unification
   - Generates sub-obligations that must also be sati
... (truncated)

The `ObligationForest` is a utility data structure used in trait\\n2: //! matching to track the set of outstanding obligations (those not yet\\n3: //! resolved to success or error). It also tracks the \\\"backtrace\\\" of each\\n4: //! pending obligation (why we are trying to figure this out in the first\\n5: //! place).\\n6: //!\\n7: //! ### External view\\n8: //!\\n9: //! `ObligationForest` supports two main public operations (there are a\\n10: //! few others not discussed here):\\n11: //!\\n12: //! 1. Add a new root obligations (`register_obligation`).\\n13: //! 2. Process the pending obligations (`process_obligations`).\\n14: //!\\n15: //! When a new obligation `N` is added, it becomes the root of an\\n16: //! obligation tree. This tree can also carry some per-tree state `T`,\\n17: //! which is given at the same time. This tree is a singleton to start, so\\n18: //! `N` is both the root and the only leaf. Each time the\\n19: //! `process_obligations` method is called, it will invoke its callback\\n20: //! with every pending obligation (so that will include `N`, the first\\n21: //! time). The callback also receives a (mutable) reference to the\\n22: //! per-tree state `T`. The callback should process the obligation `O`\\n23: //! that it is given and return a `ProcessResult`:\\n24: //!\\n25: //! - `Unchanged` -> ambiguous result. Obligation was neither a success\\n26: //!   nor a failure. It is assumed that further attempts to process the\\n27: //!   obligation will yield the same result unless something in the\\n28: //!   surrounding environment changes.\\n29: //! - `Changed(C)` - the obligation was *shallowly successful*. The\\n30: //!   vector `C` is a list of subobligations. The meaning of this is that\\n31: //!   `O` was successful on the assumption that all the obligations in `C`\\n32: //!   are also successful. Therefore, `O` is only considered a \\\"true\\\"\\n33: //!   success if `C` is empty. Otherwise, `O` is put into a suspended\\n34: //!   state and the obligations in `C` become the new pending\\n35: //!   obligations. They will be processed the next time you call\\n36: //!   `process_obligations`.\\n37: //! - `Error(E)` -> obligation failed with error `E`. We will collect this\\n38: //!   error and return it from `process_obligations`, along with the\\n39: //!   \\\"backtrace\\\" of obligations (that is, the list of obligations up to\\n40: //!   and including the root of the failed obligation). No further\\n41: //!   obligations from that same tree will be processed, since the tree is\\n42: //!   now considered to be in error.\\n43: //!\\n44: //! When the call to `process_obligations` completes, you get back an `Outcome`,\\n45: //! which includes two bits of information:\\n46: //!\\n47: //! - `completed`: a list of obligations where processing was fully\\n48: //!   completed without error (meaning that all transitive subobligations\\n49: //!   have also been completed). So, for example, if the callback from\\n50: //!   `process_obligations` returns `Changed(C)` for some obligation `O`,\\n51: //!   then `O` will be considered completed right away if `C` is the\\n52: //!   empty vector. Otherwise it will only be considered completed once\\n53: //!   all the obligations in `C` have been found completed.\\n54: //! - `errors`: a list of errors that occurred and associated backtraces\\n55: //!   at the time of error, which can be used to give context to the user.\\n56: //!\\n57: //! Upon completion, none of the existing obligations were *shallowly\\n58: //! successful* (that is, no callback returned `Changed(_)`). This implies that\\n59: //! all obligations were either errors or returned an ambiguous result.\\n60: //!\\n61: //! ### Implementation details\\n62: //!\\n63: //! For the most part, comments specific to the implementation are in the\\n64: //! code. This file only contains a very high-level overview. Basically,\\n65: //! the forest is stored in a vector. Each element of the vector is a node\\n66: //! in some tree. Each node in the vector has the index of its dependents,\\n67: //! including the first dependent which is known as the parent. It also\\n68: //! has a current state, described by `NodeState`. After each processing\\n69: //! step, we compress the vector to remove completed and error nodes, which\\n70: //! aren't needed anymore.\\n71: \\n72: use std::cell::Cell;\\n73: use std::collections::hash_map::Entry;\\n74: use std::fmt::Debug;\\n75: use std::hash;\\n76: use std::marker::PhantomData;\\n77: \\n78: use thin_vec::ThinVec;\\n79: use tracing::debug;\\n80: \\n81: use crate::fx::{FxHashMap, FxHashSet};\\n82: \\n83: mod graphviz;\\n84: \\n85: #[cfg(test)]\\n86: mod tests;\\n87: \\n88: pub trait ForestObligation: Clone + Debug {\\n89:     type CacheKey: Clone + hash::Hash + Eq + Debug;\\n90: \\n91:     /// Converts this `ForestObligation` suitable for use as a cache key.\\n92:     /// If two distinct `ForestObligations`s return the same cache key,\\n93:     /// then it must be sound to use the result of processing one obligation\\n94:     /// (e.g. success for error) for the other obligation\\n95:     fn as_cache_key(&self) -> Self::CacheKey;\\n96: }\\n97: \\n98: pub trait ObligationProcessor {\\n99:     type Obligation: ForestObligation;\\n100:     type Error: Debug;\\n101: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7@525258043d7f9dcc1b69d9abcb9bfea32826a219/-/blob/compiler/rustc_data_structures/src/obligation_forest/mod.rs%3FL1-100\"}",

    "content": "1: //! It also tracks the \"backtrace\" of each\n4: //! pending obligation (why we are trying to figure this out in the first\n5: //! place).\n6: //!\n7: //! ### External view\n8: //!\n9: //! `ObligationForest` supports two main public operations (there are a\n10: //! few others not discussed here):\n11: //!\n12: //! 1. Obligation was neither a success\n26: //!   nor a failure. It is assumed that further attempts to process the\n27: //!   obligation will yield the same result unless something in the\n28: //!   surrounding environment changes.\n29: //! - `Changed(C)` - the obligation was *shallowly successful*. The\n30: //!   vector `C` is a list of subobligations. The meaning of this is that\n31: //!   `O` was successful on the assumption that all the obligations in `C`\n32: //!   are also successful. Therefore, `O` is only considered a \"true\"\n33: //!   success if `C` is empty. Otherwise, `O` is put into a suspended\n34: //!   state and the obligations in `C` become the new pending\n35: //!   obligations. They will be processed the next time you call\n36: //!   `process_obligations`.\n37: //! - `Error(E)` -> obligation failed with error `E`. We will collect this\n38: //!   error and return it from `process_obligations`, along with the\n39: //!   \"backtrace\" of obligations (that is, the list of obligations up to\n40: //!   and including the root of the failed obligation). No further\n41: //!   obligations from that same tree will be processed, since the tree is\n42: //!   now considered to be in error.\n43: //!\n44: //! When the call to `process_obligations` completes, you get back an `Outcome`,\n45: //! which includes two bits of information:\n46: //!\n47: //! - `completed`: a list of obligations where processing was fully\n48: //!   completed without error (meaning that all transitive subobligations\n49: //!   have also been completed). So, for example, if the callback from\n50: //!   `process_obligations` returns `Changed(C)` for some obligation `O`,\n51: //!   then `O` will be considered completed right away if `C` is the\n52: //!   empty vector. Otherwise it will only be considered completed once\n53: //!   all the obligations in `C` have been found completed.\n54: //! - `errors`: a list of errors that occurred and associated backtraces\n55: //!   at the time of error, which can be used to give context to the user.\n56: //!\n57: //! Upon completion, none of the existing obligations were *shallowly\n58: //! successful* (that is, no callback returned `Changed(_)`). This implies that\n59: //! all obligations were either errors or returned an ambiguous result.\n60: //!\n61: //! ### Implementation details\n62: //!\n63: //! For the most part, comments specific to the implementation are in the\n64: //! code. This file only contains a very high-level overview. Basically,\n65: //! the forest is stored in a vector. Each element of the vector is a node\n66: //! in some tree. Each node in the vector has the index of its dependents,\n67: //! including the first dependent which is known as the parent. It also\n68: //! has a current state, described by `NodeState`. After each processing\n69: //! step, we compress the vector to remove completed and error nodes, which\n70: //! aren't needed anymore.\n71: \n72: use std::cell::Cell;\n73: use std::collections::hash_map::Entry;\n74: use std::fmt::Debug;\n75: use std::hash;\n76: use std::marker::PhantomData;\n77: \n78: use thin_vec::ThinVec;\n79: use tracing::debug;\n80: \n81: use crate::fx::{FxHashMap, FxHashSet};\n82: \n83: mod graphviz;\n84: \n85: #[cfg(test)]\n86: mod tests;\n87: \n88: pub trait ForestObligation: Clone + Debug {\n89:     type CacheKey: Clone + hash::Hash + Eq + Debug;\n90: \n91:     /// Converts this `ForestObligation` suitable for use as a cache key.\n92:     /// If two distinct `ForestObligations`s return the same cache key,\n93:     /// then it must be sound to use the result of processing one obligation\n94:     /// (e.g. success for error) for the other obligation\n95:     fn as_cache_key(&self) -> Self::CacheKey;\n96: }\n97: \n98: pub trait ObligationProcessor {\n99:     type Obligation: ForestObligation;\n100:     type Error: Debug;\n101: ",

It maintains a tree-based structure where each obligation can have sub-obligations that must be satisfied for the parent obligation to succeed. The forest supports two main operations: registering obligations via `register_obligation()` and processing them via `process_obligations()`. The `ForestObligation` trait defines what types can be stored in the forest, while `ObligationProcessor` defines how to process them, enabling different strategies for different obligation types.\\n\\n### 2. Trait Selection and Candidate Collection\\n**Files:**\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/mod.rs` - SelectionContext definition\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/candidate_assembly.rs` - Candidate assembly logic\\n\\nThe `SelectionContext` is the heart of trait resolution. Its `assemble_candidates()` method in candidate_assembly.rs collects all possible implementations that might satisfy a trait obligation:\\n- **Trait aliases** via `assemble_candidates_for_trait_alias()`\\n- **Impl candidates** via `assemble_candidates_from_impls()` - searches the trait impl database\\n- **Caller bounds** via `assemble_candidates_from_caller_bounds()` - checks where-clauses and environment\\n- **Builtin candidates** for language items like `Sized`, `Copy`, `Clone`\\n- **Project candidates** for associated types\\n- **Object, closure, generator, and function pointer candidates**\\n\\nThe `assemble_candidates_from_impls()` function performs a deep rejection check using `DeepRejectCtxt` for quick filtering, then calls `match_impl()` for each potentially applicable impl.\\n\\n### 4. Impl Matching (Pattern Matching)\\n**File:** `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/_match.rs`\\n\\nThe `MatchAgainstFreshVars<'tcx>` struct implements `TypeRelation` to perform pattern matching between obligation types and impl types. It uses fresh type variables to check if types could potentially unify without affecting the current inference state. This bidirectional matching allows the compiler to quickly determine if an impl could apply before committing to full unification.\\n\\n### 5. Candidate Confirmation\\n**File:** `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/confirmation.rs`\\n\\nOnce a candidate is selected, `confirm_candidate()` verifies the selection by:\\n- Confirming builtin candidates via `confirm_builtin_candidate()`\\n- Confirming impl candidates via `confirm_impl_candidate()` which performs full unification\\n- Confirming parameter candidates, projection candidates, and special candidates (closures, coroutines, etc.)\\n\\nEach confirmation may generate new sub-obligations that must also be satisfied.\\n\\n### 6. It:\\n- Determines the error type (Unimplemented, Overflow, Ambiguous, etc.)\\n- Provides contextual error messages based on the obligation cause\\n- Suggests potential fixes (importing traits, fixing type annotations, etc.)\\n- Reports related trait implementations that almost matched\\n\\n### 7. New Trait Solver Integration\\n**Files:**\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/solve/fulfill.rs` - New FulfillmentCtxt\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/solve/fulfill/derive_errors.rs` - Error derivation\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/solve/select.rs` - Selection in new solver\\n\\nThe newer trait solver provides an alternative implementation with improved performance and correctness. The `fulfillment_error_for_no_solution()` and `fulfillment_error_for_stalled()` functions in derive_errors.rs generate appropriate error codes from solver results, including detection of higher-ranked trait bound violations and projection mismatches.\\n\\n### The Complete Chain:\\n\\n1. **ObligationForest** (`rustc_data_structures`) - stores obligation tree structure\\n2. **FulfillmentContext** (`rustc_trait_selection/fulfill.rs`) - manages overall fulfillment\\n3. **FulfillProcessor** - processes obligations through the forest\\n4. **SelectionContext** (`rustc_trait_selection/select/mod.rs`) - driven by FulfillProcessor\\n5. **assemble_candidates()** - collects candidate implementations\\n6. **assemble_candidates_from_impls()** - searches trait impl database  \\n7. **MatchAgainstFreshVars** - performs pattern matching on impl signatures\\n8. **confirm_candidate()** - validates selected candidate through unification\\n9. **report_selection_error()** - reports errors for unsatisfied bounds\\n\\nFor the newer solver path, the chain is:\\n1. **FulfillmentCtxt** (solve/fulfill.rs) - new solver's fulfillment\\n2. **SolverDelegate** - bridges to the core solver\\n3. **fulfillment_error_for_no_solution()** - derives errors from solver results\\n\\nThis design enables efficient trait resolution with good error messages while supporting both incremental obligation processing and complete error recovery.\"\n}\n",

Obligation Storage and Forest (ObligationForest)\\n**File:** `compiler/rustc_data_structures/src/obligation_forest/mod.rs`\\n\\nThe `ObligationForest<O>` is the core data structure that tracks outstanding trait obligations. It maintains a tree-based structure where each obligation can have sub-obligations that must be satisfied for the parent obligation to succeed. The forest supports two main operations: registering obligations via `register_obligation()` and processing them via `process_obligations()`. The `ForestObligation` trait defines what types can be stored in the forest, while `ObligationProcessor` defines how to process them, enabling different strategies for different obligation types.\\n\\n### 2. Fulfillment Context (Old Solver)\\n**File:** `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/fulfill.rs`\\n\\nThe `FulfillmentContext<'tcx, E>` manages the fulfillment process using the ObligationForest. Trait Selection and Candidate Collection\\n**Files:**\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/mod.rs` - SelectionContext definition\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/candidate_assembly.rs` - Candidate assembly logic\\n\\nThe `SelectionContext` is the heart of trait resolution. Its `assemble_candidates()` method in candidate_assembly.rs collects all possible implementations that might satisfy a trait obligation:\\n- **Trait aliases** via `assemble_candidates_for_trait_alias()`\\n- **Impl candidates** via `assemble_candidates_from_impls()` - searches the trait impl database\\n- **Caller bounds** via `assemble_candidates_from_caller_bounds()` - checks where-clauses and environment\\n- **Builtin candidates** for language items like `Sized`, `Copy`, `Clone`\\n- **Project candidates** for associated types\\n- **Object, closure, generator, and function pointer candidates**\\n\\nThe `assemble_candidates_from_impls()` function performs a deep rejection check using `DeepRejectCtxt` for quick filtering, then calls `match_impl()` for each potentially applicable impl.\\n\\n### 4. Impl Matching (Pattern Matching)\\n**File:** `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/_match.rs`\\n\\nThe `MatchAgainstFreshVars<'tcx>` struct implements `TypeRelation` to perform pattern matching between obligation types and impl types. It uses fresh type variables to check if types could potentially unify without affecting the current inference state. This bidirectional matching allows the compiler to quickly determine if an impl could apply before committing to full unification.\\n\\n### 5. Candidate Confirmation\\n**File:** `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/traits/select/confirmation.rs`\\n\\nOnce a candidate is selected, `confirm_candidate()` verifies the selection by:\\n- Confirming builtin candidates via `confirm_builtin_candidate()`\\n- Confirming impl candidates via `confirm_impl_candidate()` which performs full unification\\n- Confirming parameter candidates, projection candidates, and special candidates (closures, coroutines, etc.)\\n\\nEach confirmation may generate new sub-obligations that must also be satisfied.\\n\\n### 6. Error Reporting for Unsatisfied Bounds\\n**File:** `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/error_reporting/traits/fulfillment_errors.rs`\\n\\nThe `TypeErrCtxt::report_selection_error()` method handles reporting when a trait obligation cannot be satisfied. It:\\n- Determines the error type (Unimplemented, Overflow, Ambiguous, etc.)\\n- Provides contextual error messages based on the obligation cause\\n- Suggests potential fixes (importing traits, fixing type annotations, etc.)\\n- Reports related trait implementations that almost matched\\n\\n### 7. New Trait Solver Integration\\n**Files:**\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/solve/fulfill.rs` - New FulfillmentCtxt\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/solve/fulfill/derive_errors.rs` - Error derivation\\n- `compiler/rustc_trait_selection/src/solve/select.rs` - Selection in new solver\\n\\nThe newer trait solver provides an alternative implementation with improved performance and correctness. The `FulfillmentCtxt` in solve/fulfill.rs uses the next-gen solver engine from `rustc_next_trait_solver`. The `fulfillment_error_for_no_solution()` and `fulfillment_error_for_stalled()` functions in derive_errors.rs generate appropriate error codes from solver results, including detection of higher-ranked trait bound violations and projection mismatches.\\n\\n### The Complete Chain:\\n\\n1. **ObligationForest** (`rustc_data_structures`) - stores obligation tree structure\\n2. **FulfillmentContext** (`rustc_trait_selection/fulfill.rs`) - manages overall fulfillment\\n3. **FulfillProcessor** - processes obligations through the forest\\n4. **SelectionContext** (`rustc_trait_selection/select/mod.rs`) - driven by FulfillProcessor\\n5. **assemble_candidates()** - collects candidate implementations\\n6. **assemble_candidates_from_impls()** - searches trait impl database  \\n7. **MatchAgainstFreshVars** - performs pattern matching on impl signatures\\n8. **confirm_candidate()** - validates selected candidate through unification\\n9. **report_selection_error()** - reports errors for unsatisfied bounds\\n\\nFor the newer solver path, the chain is:\\n1. **FulfillmentCtxt** (solve/fulfill.rs) - new solver's fulfillment\\n2. **SolverDelegate** - bridges to the core solver\\n3. **fulfillment_error_for_no_solution()** - derives errors from solver results\\n\\nThis design enables efficient trait resolution with good error messages while supporting both incremental obligation processing and complete error recovery.\"\n}\n",
